5. Re: Christmas Challenge FMC
From: "Kenneth Gustavsson" <solkoll@...>
To: fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com
Date: Thu, 05 Jan 2006 16:37:10 -0000

Hello Gilles =) I tried something a bit similair to your methods first step a while ago. I start with a "cross" of three edges (one from FL and two from ML) on one of the sides (R,F,L or B) then I add two FL-corners and two FL-edges to build a 2x2x3. This can be done in a mass of ways (pair up and insert like CFOP-F2L for example) and often it ends in rather few moves like 12-13 (well, not always but often).

8. Re: wca regulations on stickers
From: "Per Kristen Fredlund" <aspiring_to_love@...>
To: fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com
Date: Fri, 06 Jan 2006 16:39:52 -0000

Hi :-) I use that or else i use white paper and a tape loop made from 5 cm wide clear tape. The latter is much more durable and sticks well even on the Studio cube stickers. Post-it stickers do not stick well on the Studio cube stickers for some reason :-( I normally hardly ever use more than 5 extra numbered stickers at a time. That caters for corner 5-cycle and also corner 3-cycle and a single twisted corner. For both those cases i use/need 5 stickers. When inserting both a corner 3-cycle and edge 3-cycle i can do them independently :-) Cheers! -Per PS! For this kind of edge 5-cycle M D M' D' also 5 stickers only is needed. Make a drawing how the stickers should be placed (or use a second cube) when finding the required setup moves. > --- In fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com, "c_w_tsai" <c_w_tsai@y...> wrote: > > Has anybody seen the wca regulations? It says that competitors are > allowed 10 stickers. I think it's a little funny that there's a limit. > I can't imagine that they would strictly enforce this rule... Anyway, > 10 is probably enough. And if you ran out, you could do something like > use permanant marker on your cube to get around this rule. > > Hey, I have a questions for the other guys who insert algorithms: What > do you use for stickers? I use bits of Post-it notes, torn from the > sticky strip. They're plentiful, easy to write on, and easy to remove > afterwards. If I made them small enough I bet I could get 20+ from a > single post-it note :) >

But 38 would make me "Advanced". Can we submit several solutions and Dan takes our score for each metric from our best solution for that metric? Or can we just submit one? In that case, which one should I submit? Cheers! Stefan

Hi, FMcubers, I'm back here and I'm wondering how everybody did in the Christmas Challenge :-) Per, in your last case with 3-cycles of both edges and corners, I prefer to mark all 6 cubies for a simple reason that I preferably look for 4 moves for solving edges like U'R U R' and such. It also permutes 4 corners three of which may be the 3 not yet solved. Sometimes one additional move is enough to position the corners right. It's quite likely that I may end up with some other 3-cycle of corners. 15. Re: FMC metric importance ?
From: "Per Kristen Fredlund" <aspiring_to_love@...>
To: fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com
Date: Sun, 08 Jan 2006 15:00:18 -0000

Hi Stefan :-) For now Dan is only rating in htm :-) He has promised (i think he has ...) to switch to rating in all metrics soon, so for ur rating u should then submit the 38 htm solution, putting the second solution in the quote of ur solution ;-) My preferred metric however is stm now. I consider it to be most fair, and also it's always the shortest one ;-) Another not often discussed metric is the axial metric. For every axial used increase count with one. So something (familiar) like : (F B R L)*3 will have length 6 in that metric. F B are on same axis, same with R L so F B R L is length 2, and repeated 3 times gives 6 in total. This metric is somewhat similar to Chris Hardwick's metric for larger cube. He counts chunks of adjacent-layer moves as only 1 move. However long we discuss metrics i don't think everyone will ever agree what is the best ;-) Cheers! -Per > --- In fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com, "Stefan Pochmann" <pochmann@g...> wrote: > > What is more important in Dan's FMC? HTM or QTM? Let's say I have > these two solutions: > > 1) 38 HTM, 46 QTM > 2) 40 HTM, 44 QTM > > I like 2) better, because of few double turns and thus low QTM. > But 38 would make me "Advanced". > > Can we submit several solutions and Dan takes our score for each > metric from our best solution for that metric? Or can we just submit > one? In that case, which one should I submit? > > Cheers! > Stefan >
16. Re: FMC metric importance ?
From: "Stefan Pochmann" <pochmann@...>
To: fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com
Date: Sun, 08 Jan 2006 16:28:02 -0000

--- In fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com, "Per Kristen Fredlund" <aspiring_to_love@y...> wrote: > > For now Dan is only rating in htm :-) He has promised (i think he > has ...) to switch to rating in all metrics soon, so for ur rating u > should then submit the 38 htm solution, putting the second solution > in the quote of ur solution ;-) Alright, I'll do so then... thanks! > Another not often discussed metric is the axial metric. For every > axial used increase count with one. So something (familiar) like : > > (F B R L)*3 > > will have length 6 in that metric. F B are on same axis, same with R > L so F B R L is length 2, and repeated 3 times gives 6 in total. > This metric is somewhat similar to Chris Hardwick's metric for > larger cube. He counts chunks of adjacent-layer moves as only 1 move. Mmh... I don't think that's similar. He moves one block relative to the rest of the cube. In that axial metric, you have 3 parts moving relative to each other. That's a big difference in my opinion. I think of a move as a part of the cube standing still, and moving another part. I'm always moving one part at a time. Axial metric moves two parts at the same time, and my brain doesn't like to do more than one thing at a time :-) Cheers! Stefan

Hi, congratulations to Per for his incredible 19-move. This is a new unoficial world record. I later realized my mistake of overlooking quite obvious finish of my 11-move start. Why I didn't see this? R' B U'D F L F2 L' R' D2 R_ U F2 U' F2 U' R2 U R2 U' (20,26,19) or R' B U'D F L F2 L' R' D2 R_ D R2 U' R2 D' R2 U R2 U' (20,26,19) 26 QTM would be the shortest in this round. Mirek

Hi :-) The same scrambling technique has also given some of the worst (toughest) scrambles i have seen. Im only happy that there is a huge variety in the "complexity" of the scrambles for the FMC. Is it really desired to have some kinda quality control over the scrambles? How to do that? Who will do it? I think rigorous control over the complexity of the scrambles will make it less fun. It's nice to know that the scramble is indeed random and that sometimes real easy solutions are possible. Unusual as a 3-move roux block or 6-move 2x2x3 might be it doesn't make the scramble useless. 30. Re: #101 corners strange solutions
From: "Stefan Pochmann" <pochmann@...>
To: fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com
Date: Mon, 16 Jan 2006 12:29:54 -0000

Well, I think "corners only" should *always* include the centers. Otherwise the name is bad because then it's no need to talk about the corners of a 3x3, instead the 2x2 cube should be used. Actually I'd prefer to call it "corners + centers". And if Dan wants to keep requiring centers for Roux blocks, it should be "Roux blocks + centers". Ok ok, when you explain the cube to little dumb kids it might be helpful to tell them the centers are something special they can hold on to. But I like to think of it as just one out of 21 pieces that I have to match. Is a 40x25 pieces jigsaw puzzle called "999 pieces puzzle" because you have to solve 999 relative to one certain one? No. So "centers" should be explicitly mentioned if they shall be solved. Cheers! Stefan --- In fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com, "Per Kristen Fredlund" <aspiring_to_love@y...> wrote: > > Hi :-) > > LOL yes !! As long as the requirement is to solve wrt the centers as > well .. and not just corners wrt each other ;-) > > -Per > > > --- In fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com, "Stefan Pochmann" > <pochmann@g...> wrote: > > > > --- In fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com, "Cyril Castella" > > <cyrilca@c...> wrote: > > > > > > perhaps we should > > > use only (URF) moves for the next corners only challenge, that > would > > > make things clearer ! > > > > It would also make things "impossibler" unless the DLB corner is > > already solved :-) > > > > Cheers! > > Stefan > > >

36. Re: Scrambles
From: "Gilles Roux" <grrroux@...>
To: fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com
Date: Fri, 20 Jan 2006 17:44:56 -0000

--- In fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com, Richard Patterson <richy_jr_2000@y...> wrote: > > Good question... > > Also, When are we going to have the opportunity to set > world records in all metrics? Even if HTM is primary > for placing people at tournaments, everyone should > have equal opportunity, regardless of method. > Obviously CF and Roux methods are horrible now for a > WR attempt because of metric bias. We should not choose a metric because it gives equal chances to all methods, but because it gives the best definition of what a basic move is (many criteria, cognitive and mathematical). By the way, WCA regulations v2006 are out, and the official metric remains HTM. This time, I didn't say a word on this subject. Because I may appear as having biased ideas about it, and because as a board member I want to be objective. But I regret everybody seem ok with this rule. Gilles.

I'd vote for STM anytime... like I did in the poll last year (http: //tinyurl.com/ce8fo in case someone doesn't know). It just doesn't seem to have much effect (STM was the winner by far), I guess we'll have to live with HTM forever. One reason could be simplicity, HTM can't be ignored cause most people use it, and maybe it's too complicated to have two different metrics in the rules? It's used for scrambling, and for example for Megaminx there isn't really an alternative (except fifth turn metric) since there are no inner slices. Ok, I hereby suggest the "new" metric "LTM". It's the same as STM but means "layer turn metric". Just because people think "slice" = "inner slice", which is not the case for "layer". Or maybe we'll just have to win the next WC (speed 3x3 I mean) with Roux, Corners first or similar, something where LTM appears more natural/obvious... Cheers! Stefan

39. RE: [fewestmoveschallenge] Re: Scrambles
From: "Mike Bennett" <mikebennett_one@...>
To: fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com
Date: Fri, 20 Jan 2006 13:30:00 -0600

I think HTM is definitely biased toward a specific type of solution. As someone who greatly prefers finishing with edges, it really limits what I'm able to do. I'm glad the normal speedsolving rules have changed so that slice moves are considered for +2 penalties instead of DNF's now, but I'm unsure why FMC wasn't impacted by this. The logic behind allowing slightly misalinged slices is that for methods that aren't standard CFOP, slices are used far more frequently as single moves, so they should be considered as such when determining if the puzzle is solved. In FMC, what counts as solved hasn't changed, but slice moves are no longer considered valid moves. Why is that? As it seems to me, this would equalize different methods for FMC and allow more people and solutions opportunities, but that's not the point. The point is, slices count as a single move in every other aspect of cubing, and I don't understand why they don't for FMC. I know it seems baised to say this, because I greatly prefer STM, but I really can't see why slices are counted as two moves. Anti-slice moves and unequal slice moves (LR2) still seem like separate moves to me, so axial metric would be unfair (sorry Per), but HTM is pretty much forcing people to adopt a specific type of solution to be competitive in FMC at all. That's equivalent to forcing every speedcuber to use Fridrich (which it also seems like sometimes). Just my thoughts on the subject... -Mike > > --- In fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com, "Gilles Roux" ><grrroux@f. > > > > > > But I regret everybody seem ok with this rule. > > > > > > Gilles. _________________________________________________________________ On the road to retirement? Hi all, Which metric is the best / fairest / most honest / smartest ? I don't think we should discuss that subject again here ;-) As Gilles said in his last post, we spent a huge amount of time discussing the pros and cons of each metric, we voted, and submitted the result to WCA ears . .. They decided to keep HTM, ok. We can discuss again and agree that another should be chosen (I voted for STM as well), but I doubt it would change anything ... However, I wanted to react to Mike's post. I do NOT think that choosing HTM forces any of us to stick to a unique method ... and to which one, by the way ? Most of the submitted solutions are optimized Petrus-like solves, but Sebastian and Charles have a different way for solving the cube after the initial 2x2x3. Mirek's sense of anticipation and QTM optimization is amazing and his solutions are quite far from classical petrus approaches. Zbigniew posts nearly always sub-40 solutions with his speed mehod. Per introduced his edges-first solutions a few months ago and now he has demonstrated that it is a really powerful approach ... all these approaches are derived from speedcubing methods, but they were re-optimized in order to match FMC regulations. Gilles himself is getting scores in the low 30's in a few minutes, and expert solutions quite often when he takes the time to optimize his solve. Everyone is dreaming of a method that could combine both speed and FM advantages ... but I think we should not forget what the "C" of FMC stands for ... it is indeed a challenge to struggle with a scramble during hours in order to optimize a solution ;) So you Roux solvers, go for it ! There may exist a slight modification in Gilles' method (like changing the order of the steps, or permuting the L5E or L6E by inserting commutators within the previous moves, .. .) that could make you win all next FMC's ... Good luck ... ... and explain it in your next winning solutions quotes once you have found it :) cyril --- In fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com, "Mike Bennett" <mikebennett_one@h...> wrote: but HTM is pretty much forcing people to adopt a specific type of solution to be competitive in FMC at all. My initial response was not to rebirth controversy. Perhaps I was not clear in my ideas. This is my idea: * Keep HTM as the Primary metric for competitions. For specific competition rankings. (1st Place, 2nd Place, etc) This would give consistency within individual competitions I think. * Recognize a WR for each seperate metric. While a CF solver might not place first in HTM because of the nature of the solution, a WR could still be set in STM. This would at least give recognition to brilliant solutions in all metrics. 42. Re: Scrambles
From: "Per Kristen Fredlund" <aspiring_to_love@...>
To: fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com
Date: Sat, 21 Jan 2006 03:10:59 -0000

Hi :-) I never said i was in favor of the axial metric as the official metric for fewest moves competitions. I have openly favored slice turn metric as the most objective one. I mentioned the axial metric as an alternative one (theoretically) to the other 4 common metrics. That was all ;-) Quarter turn metrics have more a mathematical appeal than practical importance. A layer turn is a layer turn in my eyes, regardless if it's a half turn or a quarter turn. Htm does not honor inner slice turns, so im ending up favoring the stm. The fact that scrambling is done using solely outer layer turns is for practical reasons. Scramblers are not necessarily cubers and need something easy to relate to :-) Those who do fewest moves and judge such solutions should be (in general) more knowledgeable than the scramblers ;-) A practical problem from having stm as the official metric would be those cases where a solution is submitted that is not optimised notationwise in the metric, like for instance ... D F2 R' L B ... In my opinion it should not be the responsibility of judges/organisers to see that R' L is a slice turn. It must be the competitors responisbility (in offline competitions) to write those moves in brackets if stm was the official metric used. Alternatively, use inner slice move notation (MES) and transform the moves accordingly. Using MES we no longer have that a certain center-color is one particular layer ;-) So personally i would not use MES in my notation cause i lose the relation between color and facename. Umm, maybe except in cases of commutators where i would use say E and then followed by E' later on. Well you know what i mean im sure :D Cheers! -Per > --- In fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com, "Mike Bennett" <mikebennett_one@h...> wrote: > > I think HTM is definitely biased toward a specific type of solution. 44. Re: [fewestmoveschallenge] Re: Scrambles
From: "Ron van Bruchem" <ron@...>
To: <fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com>
Date: Sat, 21 Jan 2006 08:59:29 +0100

Hi friends, This discussion keeps going on and on. :-) Please give me a better argument than not to favor systems. Because I feel that you favor slice systems if you use STM. :-) Look at almost all scientific stuff on Rubik's cube. You hardly see slices counted as 1 move. QTM would be a more scientific approach, and HTM is nicely in the middle. Would antislices be one move too (Fa <=> Fs)? If you are a true fewest moves competitor, then you just follow the rules and find a solution that fits the rules the best. That way changing from HTM to STM would actually be a bad thing, because you have so much experience now in solving HTM. :-) This discussion is a bit like: we should make the hurdles for 110 meters hurdles lower, because then people who jump backwards could also participate. :-) If the hurdles would be lower or higher, then we would have a very different event! Anyway, I am still not convinced. I am open to discussions for the 2007 version of the regulation. But we need better arguments than favoring systems. 45. Re: Scrambles
From: "Per Kristen Fredlund" <aspiring_to_love@...>
To: fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com
Date: Sat, 21 Jan 2006 13:36:54 -0000

Hi :-) Why should we bother about scientific literature? Those articles are written by mathematicians for the most part, and they are not speedcubers and certainly not fewest moves cubers ;-) The natural choice for an "objective" metric is either htm (cause everyone understands it) or stm cause i feel that metric does not really favor any particular solving system whereas htm truly does. Sorry Ron, this is the key point in this discussion. Like it or not. And who are the "we" u refer to? Surely not everyone on the WCA board has the same opinion on this? I know for sure that Gilles is the only one on the board who does fewest moves and he does not favor htm as the official metric in competitions. Well anyway, i'm fine with either htm or stm used as the official metric in competitions to decide a winner. Richard Patterson's idea about separate world records for each metric is a great idea. As he said in his post that will be a great incentive (motivation) for whichever method used. To come up with many winners (due to different metrics) would be ridiculous :D Happy cubing :-) -Per PS! I'm also in favor of using some software to determine the length of solutions in competitions. This will require that a judge copies the written solution on the paper to the software (as well as the scramble) and then both confirm the solution is correct and finds the length (in various metrics). Obviously the matter of confirming a fewest moves solution is less trivial than confirming validity of a speedsolve. Somehow there should be rules for this in the WCA fewest moves regulations. Even i benefitted from this in Orlando, writing down a solution in parts and not as a continuous sequence that makes up the full solution should not be accepted in my opinion. Well unless the solution is just doing one part after another, and there is no insertions needed to be done by a judge :-) Thinking about it, there should be a standard form for submitting the solution like on Dan's pages. One field for the solution and one field for the explanation. And so on ... :-) Only the standard form should be accepted. Not the "working papers". 46. Re: Scrambles
From: "Stefan Pochmann" <pochmann@...>
To: fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com
Date: Sat, 21 Jan 2006 14:09:05 -0000

--- In fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com, "Ron van Bruchem" <ron@s. ..> wrote: > > Hi friends, > > This discussion keeps going on and on. :-) > Please give me a better argument than not to favor systems. Look at this alg: [M D M', U] = (M D M') U (M D' M') U' I think of this as: M - UF comes down D - it gets replaced by LD M'- which goes on top instead U - prepares UR for the reverse way M D' M' U' - undo the first half. As you can see, a slice turn is *one* fundamental step in my thinking. Why on earth would I think of it as two steps? It's just one! More than most other (*) events, FMC is about thinking. So why not reflect the thinking in measurement? With HTM you're not measuring what I'm thinking, ultimately not measuring how I solve. (*) The exception is BLD, which is also a lot about thinking. But here, I claim many people (at least Chris and me for 3x3, and probably most larger cube competitors) think in terms of slices. Cheers! Stefan

48. Re: [fewestmoveschallenge] Re: Scrambles
From: Ryan Heise <rheise@...>
To: fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com
Date: Sun, 22 Jan 2006 15:52:44 +1100

When viewed as a puzzle for two hands, it is easy to see why those moves that divide the cube into two segments (e.g. quarter turns, half turns), are the least controversial of moves. They are the easiest and most natural to perform with two hands. That is the "physical" view. The "logical" view is to ignore the hands, and say that if we think of a sequence as 4 basic "logical" movements, then it consists of 4 moves. I personally can't remove myself from the physical view, because to me the cube will always be a puzzle for the hands, even if we do think about the cube logically in our heads. By way of example: - M2 is counted as 1 move in STM, but I don't see any natural way to perform it physically in 1 move with two hands. If M2 is performed as two moves, why should we count it as 1 move? - Even "M" is often performed as 2 moves, according to videos I've seen. - On a 5x5x5 cube, taking the notation R[n] (1 <= n <= 5) for a movement of the nth layer relative to face R, the sequence (R[2]2 R[4]2) (F[1]2) (R[2]2 R[4]2) (F[1]2) can be seen as 4 logical moves (that's the way you would think about it in your head, right?). However, without 5 physical hands, it would be difficult (or impossible?) to perform it in 4 physical moves. We can use whatever logic we want in our heads, but I think that any measurement of a solution should not be based on the number of logical steps (Fridrich is only 7 steps), but on the number of physical steps. For that, we need to consider the hands, and HTM seems to be one of the least controversial metrics we have for two-handed solves. Ryan
49. Re: [fewestmoveschallenge] Re: Scrambles
From: "Mike Bennett" <mikebennett_one@...>
To: fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com
Date: Sat, 21 Jan 2006 23:12:28 -0600

You can perform slice moves with one motion quite easily. M' with a finger pressing on the B sticker of DB, M as a thumb press on F of DF, etc. E and E' are easy with a thumb on FR or FL, S is similar but with fingers. As for larger cubes, I thought the general consensus counted inner slice turns as single moves. The OLL parity sounds much more daunting at 22 or more moves than 15. Again, I favor slice moves, but they are generally very easy to perform as single clicks of a finger, much like you would tap U or U'. But in FMC, it really shouldn't matter whether you can perform it well a certain way or not. I think Stefan put it a good way when he explained that it had more to do with following the thoughts of the cuber than what seems practical for actual execution. As for the ambiguities involved with submitting a solution containing slice turns, it should be left entirely up to the competitor to mark their solution in the way that leaves them with the most desirable movecount. If he submits U R L' F, for example, that should be four moves. If he were to submit U {R L'} F, that would be 3. If a speecuber can be expected to correctly stop the timer, not hit reset, wait for their cube, inspect properly, solve the cube completely, etc., why can't an FMCer be expected to write their solution properly? -Mike _________________________________________________________________ FREE pop-up blocking with the new MSN Toolbar ¤ get it now! http://toolbar.msn.click-url.com/go/onm00200415ave/direct/01/

Hi again friends, Richard: > I think there is no reason not to at least have WR > recognition for all metrics. Yes, we could. Although it is a bit strange situation: you try to solve in as few moves in HTM, which could be a longer solution in QTM. Anyway, let me know if there is a majority for this idea. Richard: > To say these are the rules, and you should change your > solution method accordingly...This is an incredibly > ludacris idea, and unquestionably discriminates > against any methods that favor slice turns. Of course setting a standard discriminates at least some other systems. That is my whole point. If the hurdles were taller, then you discriminate short people, or people jumping backwards. If the hurdles were shorter, then you discriminate people with good jumping techniques. Same goes if you change the distance between the hurdles. Mike: > You can perform slice moves with one motion quite easily. Have you ever seen a beginner do that? I think only 10% of pro cubers can actually do that. And still it would be an awkward move. Mike: > As for larger cubes, I thought the general consensus > counted inner slice turns as single moves. I don't think so. I think it goes for double slices (like Rr) nowadays, but not for internal slices (r). Not many people use an internal slice OLL parity for 4x4x4. Stefan: > Look at this alg: [M D M', U] = (M D M') U (M D' M') U' > ... > As you can see, a slice turn is *one* fundamental step in my thinking. > Why on earth would I think of it as two steps? It's just one! That is exactly what I mean. This algorithm is absolutely favoring STM! How many moves would you need in HTM? I am HTM solver, and actually I would try to not have this case at all. Because it sucks for HTM. Stefan: > More than most other (*) events, FMC is about thinking. So why not > reflect the thinking in measurement? With HTM you're not measuring > what I'm thinking, ultimately not measuring how I solve. Because you see many different ways of solving, you are a person who wants 'the hurdles to be lower'. Then you can use different jumping systems. But I am better jumper, so I want the hurdles to be higher. Seeing STM the same as HTM puts me in a disadvantage for now. Although I could prepare myself in STM, and maybe become better at it. Stefan: > They fear if they move the centers, all hell will break loose and thus > they never > even think about twisting anything that moves centers and never discover > slice turns. Indeed. Slice moves change the orientation of the cube to most HTM solvers. Very hard for them (centers fixed). On the other hand: to corners first solvers using HTM moves (RL') is very hard, because it changes orientation of the cube to them (corners fixed) No discrimination here. Per: > Why should we bother about scientific literature? Example: Singmaster was doing this before many of us were born, and don't tell me he did not make good contributions, even to your current thinking. Per: > The natural choice for an "objective" metric is either htm (cause > everyone understands it) or stm cause i feel that metric does not > really favor any particular solving system whereas htm truly does. Look at the current categories of solving methods. Then look at the average number of moves per method. In general you could say that a system depending on slice moves takes fewer moves, with the same work and memorisation. Beginners example: The Ideal solution takes 120-150 HTM. A similar corners first system takes 80-110 moves in STM. That is a huge difference. Professional example: CFOP system takes 50-60 moves HTM. Waterman system takes 40-48 moves STM. What I am trying to say is that you can achieve more with STM, so solutions are shorter. An interesting question is how many slices moves does Waterman system need? If you would count them double, then you would be close to the CFOP system. Even if you would use standard Roux system, then you would only use a few slice moves. So I don't think HTM favors systems at all, and I also think that STM does favor systems that use many slice moves. Counting slice moves as 2 moves, gives about equal chances to HTM and STM solvers. Counting slices moves as 1 move, gives better chances to STM solvers. Per: > And who are the "we" u refer to? Surely not everyone on the WCA > board has the same opinion on this? I hope that remark was not meant nastily. OF COURSE with 'we' I meant: our community. Everyone is equal here. But my goal is to come to a conclusion. That is much harder than just talking about ideas. The current choice was done because the community was not able to come to a conclusion. And still we are discussing. Should we have waited until the discussion was over, before organizing competitions? Per: > To come up with many winners (due to different metrics) would be > ridiculous :D I agree. Fewest moves is a relatively impopular event in competitions: we do not have many competitions with the event (2005: 3), and not many competitors (2005: 11) in each event. To me this doesn't sound like we need more fewest moves events with different formats. It would be great to hear ideas on how to make Fewest moves events more popular. Per: > I'm also in favor of using some software to determine the length > of solutions in competitions. > ...... > there should be a standard form for submitting the solution like on Dan's > pages. Please do a proposal. Per: > A practical problem from having stm as the official metric would > be those cases where a solution is submitted that is not optimised > notationwise in the metric, like for instance ... D F2 R' L B ... In > my opinion it should not be the responsibility of judges/organisers > to see that R' L is a slice turn. Any solution is the responsibility of the competitor. If you write RL' then it is two moves HTM and STM. Per: > Using MES we no longer have that a certain center-color is one > particular layer ;-) So personally i would not use MES in my > notation cause i lose the relation between color and facename. Please do not make it harder than it already is! :-) I haven't heard remarks about Fa and Fs in STM/HTM. Both one move or not? Anyway, how can we come to a conclusion on this subject? I wanted to post the message below, but I won't because I read Ryan's message, and it makes sense and it works for any size of cubes (even if most big cubes solvers can't agree with this metric ;-) )... ...Wait, no it can't!... ...Yes, it does!... ...No!... Ohhh, my poor head, where's the bright side of the force?!! :-) ---------------------------------------------------------------- > But we need better arguments than favoring systems. Very simple, better and old arguments: - Any child with an eye or two who sees a 3x3x3 cube for the first time can see 3 free equal slices in every direction. Slice 1, slice 2, slice 3. They all can be rotated the same way, around the very same axis, that's what makes the cube so magical and perfect. - Because corners are the pieces that generate more complexity, moving slice 2 even is less complex than moving slices 1 or 3. I agree, not favoring systems is the worst argument. When a system sucks, don't invent a metric to make it shine. Other bad arguments: Speedcubing and dexterity concerns, or taking software solvers as a reference when they decided to work with a more simple model (fixed centers, and a reduced set of moves) to make things convenient, even if solutions found are slightly longer. Keeping notation simple enough for competitions is a concern, but since all FMC competitors are experts, that should not be a problem. Considering that someone is "jumping backwards" because he does not see cube solving through a FaceTM-filter is not the right image. I would prefer another one. Because hurdles seemed to high or unatural to jump, some competitors decided it was safer and faster to jump over them with one leg only, the other leg on the side. They practiced this technique only. But other competitors decided that, after all, jumping over with the two legs was very possible and natural... hum... just another stupid image. :-) Now a fact: If some people optimize for XTM only and other people optimize for YTM only, choosing XTM, YTM or ZTM as the official metric can't make them all happy. And we need only *one* metric. But maybe you remember an idea I was suggesting last year. 0) In my opinion, the gap between the length of slice-based speedcubing methods and face-based speedcubing methods is different for fewest-moves solving (try to find a sub-25 STM solution every week using a corners-first system, if it's so easy). 1) *Pure* F(Q)TM-solving often leads to 1-2 move shorter S(Q)TM solutions. 2) An F(Q)TM-expert who decides to use a few rather basic S(Q)TM-friendly tricks could reduce the move count even more. ---> I was hoping for Face-experts to try to do their best to optimize for slices (Per, I think you tried it, your opinion?), to know how easy it is. You'll tell me that, similarly, a Face-metric could be good for a slice-expert with more efforts. That's why I was asking for a STM Cube Explorer (Kociemba did not answer). It could prove, maybe, that inner-slices are not that useful to reduce the move count, less than they are for speedcubing, if that's what's frightening people. No really, if someone proves mathematically that if, under STM, the average length of perfect STM solves is 17.1, and the average length of perfect HTM solves with unwanted lucky slice optimizations is 17.3, wouldn't it change something? Gilles. PS: Choosing whether to use a quarter-turn or half-turn based metric is another topic of course. PS2: Please don't mention anti-slice moves (by the way, the "anti" prefix is not well choosen, it's not the opposite of an inner-slice move). I may mistaken, but I think this problem has not been raised before and it leads directly to the axial metric.

57. Re: Scrambles
From: "Stefan Pochmann" <pochmann@...>
To: fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com
Date: Sun, 22 Jan 2006 17:53:11 -0000

--- In fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com, Ryan Heise <rheise@p...> wrote: > > - On a 5x5x5 cube, taking the notation R[n] (1 <= n <= 5) for a movement > of the nth layer relative to face R, the sequence (R[2]2 R[4]2) > (F[1]2) (R[2]2 R[4]2) (F[1]2) can be seen as 4 logical moves (that's > the way you would think about it in your head, right?). No, I'd consider (R[2]2 R[4]2) as two steps. But I won't consider M to be one step, cause even if I wanted to, I can't split it further. It's one atomic action for me. I have no trouble considering R[2]2 and R[4] 2 as two separate steps/actions, and in fact I do. On the other hand, I'd consider R[2-4]2 to be one single action. Mainly I like to think of an action as something moving while the rest is standing still. And here the one thing that's moving is the middle three slices. In your example, two parts are moving. > We can use whatever logic we want in our heads, but I think that any > measurement of a solution should not be based on the number of logical > steps (Fridrich is only 7 steps), but on the number of physical steps. One of the FMC rules is that you have to be able to "explain" your solution. So you can't use a Fridrich algorithm as one step unless you can explain it. And when you can explain it, you can explain its steps, cause a Fridrich alg (or pretty much any alg) is definitely not one atomic action. Cheers! Stefan
58. Re: [fewestmoveschallenge] Re: Scrambles
From: Ryan Heise <rheise@...>

On Sun, Jan 22, 2006 at 04:41:40PM -0000, Stefan Pochmann wrote: > HTM: > - You (e.g. Fridrich solver) get measured the way you solve. > - I (e.g. Waterman solver) do NOT get measured the way I solve. > > STM: > - You get measured the way you solve. > - I get measured the way I solve. As Ron pointed out, Waterman only requires about three quarters the moves of Fridrich (in "STM", that is) to solve a cube, but roughly the same number of moves in HTM. If we were to actually see a Waterman practitioner solve a cube in three quarters the time of the best Fridrich practitioner (on average), then there would be some evidence to suggest that STM measures movements of the hands comparably for Waterman and Fridrich. Presently, it would seem that HTM measures movements of the hands more comparably for Waterman and Fridrich than does STM. According to current timings, it would seem that: * the average appearance of M "in an algorithm" is slower than the average appearance of R in an algorithm. * the average appearance of M2 "in an algorithm" is slower than the average appearance of R2 in an algorithm. I can't say I know "why", just that current timings of solutions seem to suggest this. If I were to guess at "why", I would say that the problem is only slightly caused by the additional friction of movements of two joints (for a three-segment move) rather than one (for a two-segment move) and the less muscle available to force the movement (the opposable finger *and* thumb are generally not "both" available to force the movement of the middle slice, since one or the other will hold one of the outer layers to stop it from moving), since a well-lubed cube can minimise this problem. If that is only a "slight" factor, I would say that the major factor is that, since these moves require more fingers in precise locations, it can take longer to get your fingers into place before you're ready to perform a move, and longer to get them to the next place after the move. This would only be a problem when an M or M2 appears "in an algorithm" (as was the case set above). I may indeed be wrong about my guess, but it was only a guess, not the crux of my argument. The crux is that current timings suggest that HTM measurements of Fridrich and Waterman solutions are more comparable than their STM counterparts. For this, and the original reasons given, HTM seems to be a less controversial metric when considering the cube as fundamentally a physical puzzle. Two ways to go about getting STM accepted as a more accurate metric would be: 1. Convince people that the cube is not fundamentally a physical puzzle, and convince people to take an alternative view. -- or -- 2. Learn to perform the Waterman method in three quarters the speed of the fastest Fridrich practitioner, on average. Ryan
59. Re: [fewestmoveschallenge] Re: Scrambles
From: Ryan Heise <rheise@...>
To: fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com
Date: Mon, 23 Jan 2006 12:58:15 +1100

On Sun, Jan 22, 2006 at 05:53:11PM -0000, Stefan Pochmann wrote: > On the other hand, I'd consider R[2-4]2 to be one single action. > Mainly I like to think of an action as something moving while the rest > is standing still. I understand what you mean, but it is controversial because here the "rest" is actually two segments, not one. In terms of force, the two "rests" need to be forced to stay still by two separate forces. Relatively speaking, we can view this also as the middle layer staying still, and the two "rests" being pushed away by two separate forces. I'm sure you can argue on this, but I'm merely trying to point out that it is controversial. Ryan

71. Slices vs Cutplanes
From: "Gilles Roux" <grrroux@...>
To: fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com
Date: Mon, 23 Jan 2006 12:05:20 -0000

Possible definitions of a move: 1) Consider our 3D sequential puzzles are made of slices. Move one while others remain still ---> Slice(Q)TM. 2) Consider our puzzles are volumes divided by cutplanes. For a given cutplane, you can only move the 2 blocks it generates ---> Face(Q)TM (for the 3x3x3). Dual views. I respect both. Gilles.
72. Re: [fewestmoveschallenge] Re: Scrambles
From: Ryan Heise <rheise@...>
To: fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com
Date: Tue, 24 Jan 2006 00:20:14 +1100

On Mon, Jan 23, 2006 at 12:06:01AM -0800, Richard Patterson wrote: > Is there a majority for this idea? At least its a > compromise, HTM primary for competitions, but people > can still set WRs in STM. Is the STM record just for the people who care about slice moves? If so, there is very little difference in the ranking of those people between HTM and STM. In the common case, the HTM measurement of a solution is equal to the STM measurement plus the number of slice moves. In this case, the ranking stays the same. In some special cases, people who were tied will be split. Specifically, (L'R.R) and (L'R.D) are tied in STM, but the first is more efficient than the second in HTM. Maybe you can see the reason in that and live with it. If you can live with it, there is a significant advantage to choosing a HTM based ranking over a STM based ranking: the ranking would be comparable with the rest of the competitors who used strategies not based around slice moves. Ryan

Solutions that are "better" in the STM may be merely "equal" in the physical world. For example, Waterman is better than Fridrich in the STM, but roughly equal to Fridrich in the real world. Using the STM for FMC would encourage people to use slice moves for no reason. Can you give me any reason to use slice moves other than just that they score well in STM? If not, then I have no reason to use slice moves except for an artificial one. I do not buy that they are really any "better" ( - or any worse, for that matter. They are just a different kind of move that support a different kind of strategy). So why introduce a metric that would encourage people to use slice moves? Ryan

78. Re: [fewestmoveschallenge] Re: Scrambles
From: Ryan Heise <rheise@...>
To: fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com
Date: Tue, 24 Jan 2006 12:51:41 +1100

On Mon, Jan 23, 2006 at 03:03:04PM -0000, Stefan Pochmann wrote: > --- In fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com, Ryan Heise <rheise@p...> > wrote: > > > > Can you give me any reason to use slice moves other than just > > that they score well in STM? > > Believe it or not, it's just what I'm doing, and I would use inner > slice moves without knowing STM (btw, *inner*... Fridrich uses about > 55-60 *slice* moves, too, just *outer* slices). > > For speedcubing I use them because it's fast and for FMC because it's > more natural to think for example for the edge-3-cycle commutator I > showed. You haven't really answered the question, and you have removed the context in which it was asked. Let me bring it back: Why should we adopt a metric that treats slice-based methods as better than non-slice-based methods when there is good real-world evidence to suggest that they are actually roughly the same? This would eventually encourage people to incorporate slice moves into their methods for artificial reasons only. On Mon, Jan 23, 2006 at 04:05:36PM -0000, Gilles Roux wrote: > If you want a metric that's good at describing physical performance, > don't use HTM. I think I chose my words precisely enough. HTM is less controversial for this purpose than is STM. I am not proposing we invent a new metric that is perfectly accurate, I am proposing that we select one of the least controversial existing metrics. There is no strong evidence to suggest that the current HTM isn't giving slice and non-slice -based methods a level playing field. I don't see how anything can be changed if there isn't any real issue to address. Nobody has yet developed corners first methods to the level that Mirek initially developed block methods for fewest moves. Changing the metric is not the way to get corners first methods, and other slice-based methods, to catch up. It is debatable that there is really any problem to fix, but it is certain that introducing STM would create a new problem. STM would give people an artificial incentive to modify their methods to incorporate slice moves. STM by definition will allow shorter solutions that are not possible to achieve by methods that do not employ slice moves. I don't see a good reason why non-slice-based methods should lose their rating to slice-based methods through the use of a metric that rewards slice-based methods relative to non-slice-based methods, when evidence suggests that these methods are roughly equivalent. STM will allow you to get better scores by switching to a method that is equivalent. Do we really want everybody to switch to slice-based methods, when there is no real evidence to suggest that they are better? Don't we prefer diversity? Let's just keep the HTM which has historically done a good job in rating slice-based and non-slice-based methods as roughly equivalent. Ryan
79. Re: [fewestmoveschallenge] Re: Scrambles
From: Richard Patterson <richy_jr_2000@...>

82. Re: [fewestmoveschallenge] Re: Scrambles
From: Ryan Heise <rheise@...>
To: fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com
Date: Tue, 24 Jan 2006 22:19:14 +1100

On Tue, Jan 24, 2006 at 12:32:56AM -0600, Mike Bennett wrote: > But edge insertions are just as useful and relevant. I'm still at a > loss why they are being slighted. There is nothing wrong with methods that use edge insertions, but if you count slice moves as 1 move, you can achieve a move count that is lower than what is possible by a non-slice-based method. It only makes sense to allow this if you can convince us that slice methods are indeed better than non-slice methods. Do you think slice methods are inherently better than non-slice methods? Please answer that question. > I don't think block methods are more advanced than > advanced Roux or other CF methods. If you're right, then I will of course agree to use STM. Unfortunately, you haven't supplied evidence. The best winning FMC solutions in STM happen to have been developed using block methods, not slice methods, where the bonus slice moves were just a happy coincidence. Fewest moves solving for block methods has been practiced by Mirek and Lars for decades, and their wisdom kindly donated to us for free. Fewest moves solving for slice methods is relatively new, and dare I say it, has more room for improvement. Rather than try to change the metric, why not just put development efforts into your method to equal the decades of effort that have been put behind fewest moves solving with block methods? > I don't see a good reason why non-slice-based methods should lose their > rating to slice-based methods through the use of a metric that rewards > slice-based methods relative to non-slice-based methods, when evidence > suggests that these methods are roughly equivalent. STM will allow you > to get better scores by switching to a method that is equivalent. Do we > really want everybody to switch to slice-based methods, when there is no > real evidence to suggest that they are better? Don't we prefer > diversity? > > > So, you're afraid STM would take over and everyone would only use slice based > methods? How is that different than our complaint that HTM has taken over and > everyone only uses HTM based methods? The difference is that one side is backed by evidence. Let's look at the two extremes: - Suppose competitors are so good that they regularly submit optimal solutions. Under STM, it is possible that an optimal solution cannot be achieved by methods that do not employ slice moves. - Suppose competitors are new to the game use stock standard methods with memorised algorithms. Under STM, known slice-based methods are scored as better than their non-slice counterparts. It is not difficult to interpolate the middle ground. If you think that slice methods are in fact better, then fine. Let's use STM. But first you need to convince me that slice methods *are* in fact better. Then STM is justified. Ryan
83. Re: Scrambles
From: "Josef Jelinek" <josef.jelinek@...>
To: fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com
Date: Tue, 24 Jan 2006 11:53:22 -0000

Hi all, interesting arguments... We should not argue by referring to existing methods (especially the speedcubing ones) and people performance... I would like to add one point I have not seen here... Counting inner slice turn as one turn seems to me equivalent to counting centers as movable cubies... If you do that then outer slice turn = moving 4 + 4 cubie block wrt 4 + 8 + 6 cubies inner slice turn = moving 4 + 4 cubie block wrt 8 + 8 + 2 cubies It does not matter for computer programs, because they do not think and can easily convert slice turns to face turns + cube rotations and it simplifies the data structures while increasing speed... (that is why ACube cannot ignore centers (in the current version) - they are bound to cube rotation).. Btw, it should be quite easy to add slice turns to Cube Explorer this way... transformation tables would be extended to handle Xs style turns and pruning tables (the biggest memory eater) would stay the same... You can transform the output then to output real slice turns... (the same thing I did on ACube 2)... Josef

88. Re: Slices vs Cutplanes
From: "Gilles Roux" <grrroux@...>
To: fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com
Date: Tue, 24 Jan 2006 13:52:36 -0000

--- In fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com, "Gilles Roux" <grrroux@f...> wrote: > > > Possible definitions of a move: > > 1) Consider our 3D sequential puzzles are made of slices. Move one > while others remain still ---> Slice(Q)TM. > > 2) Consider our puzzles are volumes divided by cutplanes. For a given > cutplane, you can only move the 2 blocks it generates ---> Face(Q)TM > (for the 3x3x3). > > Dual views. I respect both. > > > Gilles. > Any comment about this? I think it's fundamental to focus of what a move is, cognitively and intuitively (because it's a craft, because it's a physical puzzle you have in front of you, not a virtual riddle), and scientifically. Focusing on finding a metric to make systems "equal" is a wrong debate. 1) When you "see slices", rotating around the same axis, you can think with inner slice moves naturally, you can speedcube with them, etc. There's no good reason to reject them, or consider they're complex. 2) When you "see cutplanes", they define 2 sliding blocks. If you focus too much on slices (as I was), looking at a square-1 could change your opinion. There's no metric to rule them all, and in puzzleness bind them. Gilles.

Had it been 3 corners I would've been happy, but edges? corners [L D L', U] = 8/8 in STM/HTM edges.. [M D M', U] = 8/12 in STM/HTM Notice even though it's the same idea, how much it sucks if you have to do it with edges. Edges are punished a lot with HTM, in STM it's 8 for both. There's also edges.. [U R U', M] = 8/10 in STM/HTM but that's still 10 in HTM. And yes there's also (U2 M U2 M') but do you know how lucky you need to be to find them aligned like this for free without setup moves? I ended up finding one good spot, inserting a 10-move 3-cycle luckyily cancelling 4 moves. That was by far the best insertion I found, adding 6 moves to my solution length. But whether or not it's fun, whether or not edges are discriminated, whether or not whatever, that's all not the point. The point is that HTM is not measuring what I'm doing. Stefan

91. Re: Scrambles
From: "Ryan Heise" <rheise@...>
To: fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com
Date: Tue, 24 Jan 2006 14:17:07 -0000

--- In fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com, "Stefan Pochmann" <pochmann@g...> wrote: > > corners [L D L', U] = 8/8 in STM/HTM > edges.. [M D M', U] = 8/12 in STM/HTM > > Notice even though it's the same idea, how much it sucks if you have > to do it with edges. Edges are punished a lot with HTM, in STM it's 8 > for both. You're assuming that corners and edges are the same - they're not. In a corners first, edges last system, it may take more moves to cycle edges, but it takes fewer moves to set up the corners because there are only 8 of them. In an edges first, corners last system, it may take fewer moves to cycle corners, but it takes more moves to set up the edges because there are 12 of them. Ryan

94. Re: Scrambles
From: "Per Kristen Fredlund" <aspiring_to_love@...>
To: fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com
Date: Tue, 24 Jan 2006 15:49:21 -0000

Hi :-) The choice between htm and stm (or qtm for that matter) as the official fewest move metric has nothing to do with: 1 - popularity of methods for speedcubing 2 - which method is best for speeding or fewest moves 3 - wanting to discriminate particular methods The fact remains that in my opinion a layer turn is a layer, regardless whether this is an outer or inner layer. Let's not turn this whole discussion into a political/strategical discussion about methods. Sadly it seems that some people here take this discussion very personally. Im only interested in : "what is a move on the (3x3x3) cube"? The natural way to define this is that turning an outer OR inner layer makes up a single move/turn on the cube. Corner cycles are made up (normally) of outer layer turns, while it's natural for edge cycles to be made up using inner slice turns. In fact for larger cubes using inner slice turns is the ONLY way to make edge cycles. No other turns will break the "edge-blocks". I know this is primarily a discussion about metric for 3x3x3 fewest moves. But i have to mention it :-) From that definition of what is a move i can only conclude that stm is the best, most neutral, metric to use. We also had a poll amongst those who regularly participate in the FMC challenge. The majority wanted to have stm as the official metric. I don't see why people who not do fewest moves at all should have a saying in this. Im sorry if this remark offends people. But this is how it should be in any aspect of life. Speedcubers have no need for a move metric. As mentioned elsewhere their metric is TIME, not moves :-) Kind regards, -Per > --- In fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com, "Ryan Heise" <rheise@p...> wrote: > > --- In fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com, "Stefan Pochmann" > <pochmann@g...> wrote: > > > > corners [L D L', U] = 8/8 in STM/HTM > > edges.. [M D M', U] = 8/12 in STM/HTM > > > > Notice even though it's the same idea, how much it sucks if you have > > to do it with edges. Edges are punished a lot with HTM, in STM it's 8 > > for both. > > You're assuming that corners and edges are the same - they're not. > > In a corners first, edges last system, it may take more moves to cycle > edges, but it takes fewer moves to set up the corners because there > are only 8 of them. > > In an edges first, corners last system, it may take fewer moves to > cycle corners, but it takes more moves to set up the edges because > there are 12 of them. > > Ryan >

96. Re: [fewestmoveschallenge] Re: Slices vs Cutplanes
From: Ryan Heise <rheise@...>
To: fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com
Date: Wed, 25 Jan 2006 02:50:57 +1100

On Tue, Jan 24, 2006 at 01:52:36PM -0000, Gilles Roux wrote: > I think it's fundamental to focus of what a move is, cognitively and > intuitively (because it's a craft, because it's a physical puzzle you > have in front of you, not a virtual riddle), and scientifically. Any argument that one metric is more valid than another is doomed to failure, unless we know what we are trying to measure. Are we trying to measure the length of thought, or the length of physical movement? I have suggested to ground this discussion in physical movement, and to pick a metric that most closely corresponds to time. I suggest we ground the discussion here, because physical movement is tangible, time is absolute, this is where cubing was first conceived, it is familiar, and we can compare the metric to time, something that is real. Trying to ground the discussion elsewhere leaves too many alternative and equally valid options open to debate, and none can ever be chosen over the other. > Focusing on finding a metric to make systems "equal" is a wrong debate. That debate never happened. It is, however, the case that various systems happen to be equal under both time and HTM. They were not "made" this way, they just happen to be this way. I suggest HTM as a good all round metric for anything ground in the physical side of the cube because of this nice (even if not perfect) correspondence between time and HTM. Ryan

99. STM sucks
From: "Gilles Roux" <grrroux@...>
To: fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com
Date: Tue, 24 Jan 2006 20:27:46 -0000

In front of me, some of my friends: - A 4^3, - a square-1, - a ultimate skewb, - a 3^3, - a pyramix, - and, last but not least, a megaminx. When I see them all, it now looks clear to me that they have one thing in common. *** CUTPLANES *** That's what define what they are, their identity as puzzles. -> That's what make moves possibles. <- If there's a definition a move, that's where to look at. On a 3^3, two parallel cutplanes define an inner layer that looks the same as outer layers. Nice, maybe that's why this puzzle looks so sexy. And thinking with slices can be natural and make solving easier. But if you consider an inner slice move as one only move, why not say that a megaminx has an inner layer too, even if it's a bit thick and different, and say you can move it with one move? And if " lr' " is only one move on a 4^3 even if you're moving two layers, should we say " lR' " is one move too, or two because the layers are not adjacent? Nay... There's something wrong. Considering one cutplane at a time, and moving the two blocks generated by this plane, is so easy and obvious, for any kind of puzzles. I like to be wrong, but for good reasons(*). Today, I would vote for HTM, see you tomorrow :-) Gilles. (*) != dexterity, != hurdles, != time ;-)
100. Re: STM sucks
From: "Per Kristen Fredlund" <aspiring_to_love@...>
To: fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com
Date: Tue, 24 Jan 2006 21:08:59 -0000

Hi :-) We are discussing a metric to be used for fewest moves for the 3x3x3 cube. We can discuss metric for other kinds of puzzles when fewest moves for those become a reality. I can't see that happening in the near future ;-) There's already few people doing fewest moves on the 3x3x3 even heaps of ppl do normal speedcubing with it. Outer layer moves are contained within the stm's "arsenal of moves". I'd vote like this for puzzles u have mentioned: 3x3x3 : stm 4x4x4 : chris hardwick's "stm" metric - R is 1 move, Rr is 1 move, rl' is one move, rl is 2 moves because it can't be fingertricked like one natural move (5x5x5 : same as 4x4x4) Skewb : has only one kind of possible move, no problem :-) Square-1 : i'd vote for axial metric, adjusting U and D layers (in any way possible is 1 move, flipping over is 1 move Pyraminx : see Skewb! ( i would NOT! consider adjusting the corner- tips as moves since those moves are trivial!) Megaminx : see Skewb! Let's keep this discussion to the 3x3x3 for now :-) Cheers! -Per > --- In fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com, "Gilles Roux" <grrroux@f...> wrote: > > > In front of me, some of my friends: > > - A 4^3, > - a square-1, > - a ultimate skewb, > - a 3^3, > - a pyramix, > - and, last but not least, a megaminx. > > > When I see them all, it now looks clear to me that they have one thing in common. > > *** CUTPLANES *** > > That's what define what they are, their identity as puzzles. > > -> That's what make moves possibles. <- > > If there's a definition a move, that's where to look at. > > > On a 3^3, two parallel cutplanes define an inner layer that looks the same as outer layers. Nice, maybe that's why this puzzle looks so sexy. And thinking with slices can be natural and make solving easier. > But if you consider an inner slice move as one only move, why not say that a megaminx has an inner layer too, even if it's a bit thick and different, and say you can move it with one move? > And if " lr' " is only one move on a 4^3 even if you're moving two layers, should we say " lR' " is one move too, or two because the layers are not adjacent? > Nay... There's something wrong. > > Considering one cutplane at a time, and moving the two blocks generated by this plane, is so easy and obvious, for any kind of puzzles. > > I like to be wrong, but for good reasons(*). > > Today, I would vote for HTM, see you tomorrow :-) > > Gilles. > > > (*) != dexterity, != hurdles, != time ;-) >

105. Re: [fewestmoveschallenge] STM sucks
From: Ryan Heise <rheise@...>
To: fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com
Date: Wed, 25 Jan 2006 10:54:46 +1100

On Tue, Jan 24, 2006 at 08:27:46PM -0000, Gilles Roux wrote: > I like to be wrong, but for good reasons(*). When argued only on abstract grounds, your conclusion is easily debatable, as you are already seeing. I would urge this debate to be placed on physical grounds, since that is where the cube was conceived, and that is where relevant measurements can take place. If there is no relation to "physical" cubing, what is the relevance? Since the number of hands we have affects how easy it is to perform certain moves, we should consider the hands. If humans only had one hand, STM would definitely be out. If humans had three hands, STM would possibly be in (or some other generalisation of three-segment moves). When you go back to the roots of cubing, solving with your hands, it can be verified that HTM is a good measure for two hands. (As an aside, it is easy to answer your question about "what is a valid definition of a move". We have done that and come up with several valid alternatives. So yes, while it is important to agree on what are the valid definitions, that is not the end of it all. Now we must select one, and we can't do that simply on the validity of the definitions.) Ryan

122. Re: [fewestmoveschallenge] Re: STM sucks
From: Ryan Heise <rheise@...>

On Wed, Jan 25, 2006 at 09:03:39AM -0000, Per Kristen Fredlund wrote: > Hi Ryan :-) > > Your physical world is restricted to "wrist cubing". Many cubers > don't need to transcribe M to R L'. 1. My argument is independent of notation. I am using Singmaster notation in which M is expressed as L'R (or is that M'? I never remember. At least I am sure of the direction of rotation in L'R) 2. I certainly did not restrict the physical world or invent my own. I am merely observing real world performances of L'R by expert physical performers. No matter how you perform it, there is no empirical evidence to support the view that the physical length of L'R is equal to the physical length of R. The empirical evidence suggests that L'R (or M.. or M') is double the physical length of R. 3. Please tell me physical length of a solution doesn't matter -- if that's your view. It will avoid me from having to give the same answers over and over again :-) Ryan

131. Re: Just...
From: "Ryan Heise" <rheise@...>
To: fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com
Date: Wed, 25 Jan 2006 15:57:03 -0000

> Well, you don't need to use insertions to stay competitive. From my > recent solutions, my #100 and #103 didn't use insertions and they were > 30 and 29 moves respectively. And there's certainly no rule that says > you have to use the same approach that other people use. It's not that my solutions couldn't be competitive. It's that the rules allow people to improve their solutions after their first attempt. The competition decides who is best at playing by those rules, not who can find the shortest solution after a single attempt. I would be entering a competition where the goal is to be best at something that I am not trying to be best at. What I'm saying is similar to a speedcubist being asked to enter a competition in which the rules state you are allowed to pause the stop watch to think, or to rewind the stop watch if you made a mistake. A speedcubist would not be interested in entering such a competition. This doesn't mean that that person isn't a speedcubist, and it doesn't mean that I'm not a fewest moves cubist. Ryan
132. Re: STM sucks
From: "Ryan Heise" <rheise@...>
To: fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com
Date: Wed, 25 Jan 2006 16:23:22 -0000

> a bit offtopic, addressed to Ryan: > how many Waterman practitioners you know of? Or you compare me to the > most of other cubists (that practice much more) :-(? I doubt that you > have much data to put this as a fact... If you know about other > (active) Watermanians, please, tell me.. I would not feel so alone :-). The evidence I used was not comparing you with all of the Fridrich speedcubists. That doesn't provide any useful information. Before Fridrich posted her method on the Internet so that many people could learn it, there was for the most part only one practitioner for each method - the inventor. Comparing the best slice practitioners with the best layer practitioners has not revealed at all the superiority of slice methods which STM indicates that it has. So don't worry, I wasn't trying to conclude anything from the fact that Fridrich practitioners are currently in the lead. However, present and past turns up no evidence that the short STM lengths of slice method solutions actually means anything useful in terms of the physical length of those solutions. HTM seems to do a better job when it comes to the physical length of a solution. In case people have forgotten again, this relates to FMC only if you think a measurement of a solution should be grounded in the physical context of the cube (i.e. solving it with hands) in order to be relevant. Ryan
133. Re: [fewestmoveschallenge] Re: STM sucks
From: Lars Petrus <lars@...>
To: fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com
Date: Wed, 25 Jan 2006 09:36:17 -0800

On Jan 25, 2006, at 1:53, Per Kristen Fredlund wrote: > Hi :-) > > Ladies and gentlemen ;-) > > We are never going to come to an agreement by discussing this any > further. Let us create a final poll on this matter. Anyone has > objections to that? There's also the option that WCA doesn't endorse a particular metric. If possible, I think WCA should just codify an existing consensus among cubers rather than trying to decide/dictate/predict what the Best Way is. Because that's both very hard, and also controversial. Here we have no consensus, which is why there is such a storm in this teacup. Ideally, each competition organizer could pick whatever metric they wanted, and over time a pattern would emerge. That pattern could be one universally respected metric, or several different "schools" of thought on the matter. Of course, there are almost no competitions arranged in this field, so that's a bit problematic. I would take that as an indication that a rule framework governing these non existent competitions isn't urgently needed, but I can see how you could make other arguments. As for recognizing records, I find it a bit odd with single solve FMC records, especially without regards to how much time was allowed. The discussion also made me think of some new possible metrics for competitions: -- Time FMC A: How fast can you turn in a solution shorter than X moves? -- Time FMC B: You're given X hours to find the FASTEST solution to a certain position. The one who executes their solve fastest on a stack mat after that wins. -- Classic: Fewest physical moves, done live within X minutes. I think all could be a lot of fun at an actual competition. And I'm sure we can think of more. - - - - - - - - - - - - "The mind is like a parachute. It doesn't work unless it's open." --- Frank Zappa Lars Petrus - lars@... http://lar5.com

139. FMC #105 (Blindfold FMC)
From: "Dan" <dan_j_harris@...>
To: fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com
Date: Sat, 28 Jan 2006 09:44:58 -0000

Hi everyone, It's great to see a lot of activity on the forum lately, even if it is dragging up the old chestnut of which metric to use! Of course, being the organiser of the FMC's today, I still have no idea about which metric to use ;) Maybe FMC's could be judged on the average of all 4 FMC metrics, or even the sum total. It wouldn't be too hard I'm sure for organisers to plug the solutions into Charles' FMC companion to find out the relevant move counts. I think it would be a good idea to try and standardise some FMC practices to be in line with competitions, and there is one easy thing we could implement. In most real FMC competitions you can have 60 minutes or 90 minutes to find and write down your solution. So why don't we do the same for online FMC? Most people I'm sure could find 90 minutes of their week where they could sit down and really have a go at the FMC. This would benefit those people who have busier lives and cannot spend as much time in a whole week on the FMC than others. And it would also give us lots of practice for making good solves in competition conditions. Let me know what you think. Anyhoo, I have launched FMC#105 today, and I think you will find that the idea is quite similar to some of the ideas that Lars posted earlier in the week! We shall definitely have to devote some other weeks to trying out those new variations :) But this week, the aim is to find a good FMC solution, and then time how long it takes you to memorise and execute it. The winner will be decided on the lowest total of moves + time in seconds. When memorising, be sure to take your time because you are only allowed one attempt to complete the cube and successfully complete the event. But don't go too slowly, or you won't win! And the lower you can get your move count, the easier it will be to memorise and execute quickly! The scramble is as always, posted on the FMC page. I hope you enjoy this variation, and Good Luck! Dan :)

144. Linear FMC
From: Ryan Heise <rheise@...>
To: fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com
Date: Mon, 30 Jan 2006 15:00:59 +1100

Would anyone be interested in this format? - Average of 10 solves, 10 random scrambles of 25 moves each. - 1 minute time limit per solve (1 move penalty for each extra second) - Submit just the number of moves per solve (not the actual moves) - Backtracking moves count as extra moves. - Winner has the lowest average number of moves. Ryan

153. Re: [fewestmoveschallenge] Re: Linear FMC
From: Ryan Heise <rheise@...>
To: fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com
Date: Tue, 31 Jan 2006 13:54:35 +1100

On Mon, Jan 30, 2006 at 09:24:30AM -0000, a_ooms75 wrote: > Do you get 15 sec preinspection :) > it sound more like speedcubing but i wanna try and Maybe 2 minutes is better. That seems like a lot of time to think about a single attempt. Without the ability to take back moves and try many alternatives for hours on end, I don't see the need for more time than this. Also, anything longer would make an average of 10 too tedious. Or would an average of 5 be ok? While I don't think this would really be a "speed" competition, there is certainly a connection to speed competitions: they both have the same notion of "solving". That is, you start from the beginning and your solution progresses linearly in time until you reach the final move. This means that if you improve your method for linear FMC, you may be able to transfer those method improvements over to speed competitions. The current FMC notion of solving is very different. You can construct your solution in a random order. e.g. you can start at the end, and finish at the beginning, or go from the beginning, to the end, to the middle (with backtracking and insertions). It's far removed from physical solving (and you know I'm a big advocate of the "physical" view :-) Ryan

160. Automated competition system
From: Ryan Heise <rheise@...>
To: fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com
Date: Wed, 1 Mar 2006 11:04:26 +1100

I spent the past week building an automated competition system, which takes care of things like submissions and generation of ranking tables. I made a demo of Charles' "Blitz" competition here: http://www.progsoc.uts.edu.au/~rheise/competitions/fmb/ Please continue to submit your solutions to Charles, but it would be helpful in testing this new system if you were to also submit an exact copy of your submission here. If it doesn't crash after 1 week, then maybe it will be useful in future :-) There is also a copy of the above competition with admin options open to everyone (who registers) so that you can see how it works: http://www.progsoc.uts.edu.au/~rheise/competitions/fmbopen/ You can also create your own experimental competition using the online user interface here: http://www.progsoc.uts.edu.au/~rheise/competitions/ Please let me know if there are any bugs (or feature requests) before I let the main cube list know :-) ** About the system ** A competition holds "tournaments". In the Blitz example above, the current tournament will run over 20 weeks, with one new challenge each week. As each challenge closes and the next one starts, the rankings table is updated to reflect the current points tally. The person with highest points tally at the end of the tournament is the tournament winner. The administrator for the competition may start a new tournament once the current one is finished, and a history of all previous tournament rankings is kept in the database (the actual challenge results are also in the database, but I haven't built the user interface to show them yet). This means that if you are the champion of the Summer tournament of 2006, you will still be remembered as such in the future. Ryan P.S. I'm sure I'm releasing this prematurely (I haven't done anywhere near enough testing), but it will still be helpful for other people to discover and report bugs.

The other formats do not > honor iterative solving style that distinguishes fewest moves from > normal solving. I would say that "iterative" and "linear" are kinds of solving, while "speed" and "fewest moves" are goals. Then "blindfolded" and "pre-inspection" are just additional attributes which can be combined. So we could have: - iterative fewest moves - linear fewest moves - linear blindfolded fewest moves - iterative speed + fewest moves - linear speed + fewest moves - etc. What distinguishes fewest moves from anything else is "fewest moves", not "iterative" :-) I think there are many people who stumbled upon linear fewest moves solving before iterative fewest moves solving (and maybe vice versa), but I accept that they both fall under the umbrella of fewest moves. I agree that different people are interested in different competitions. Maybe it would be good not to mix those 'impure' competitions into the FMC, and keep FMC classic? Other competitions could be set up for linear FMC (e.g. fewestmoves5) and linear blindfolded fewest moves. Ryan

184. Re: [fewestmoveschallenge] Automated competition system
From: Ryan Heise <ryan@...>
To: fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com
Date: Fri, 12 May 2006 09:28:29 +1000

On Thu, May 11, 2006 at 03:20:16PM -0000, Per Kristen Fredlund wrote: > Hi Dan :-) > > When will u launch a new FMC challenge? > > Or else i could make a new automated periodic system. And have a new > challenge automatically appear every week or every 14 days or > something. Hi Per, don't forget, I just created such a system: http://www.ryanheise.com/competitions/ Current features: - automatically releases new challenges periodically - automatically closes off challenges after the deadline - automatically compiles the results/rankings tables each period - keeps archives of past results/rankings - handles login/authentication - provides administrator UI to the owner of each competition - it is generic, it allows you to create new custom competitions through the user interface, following user-defined rules. For example, the rules for selecting the winner are completely user-definable through the admin user interface. I would like to also allow different point systems to be chosen. Currently, every competition uses the following point system: 1st place: 100 points 2nd place: 90 points 3rd place: 81 points etc. I think most competitions would be happy with that. (fewestmoves5 used a different point system, but I have since changed it) The advantage of a points system for fewest moves challenges as with other sports, is that some days will be easier than others. E.g. in running, some days the wind can assist you, others it can hold you back. Some days, you will get an easy cube scramble, others it will be difficult (for humans). A points system assumes that everyone gets the same advantage (i.e. same conditions), and only considers who was better than whom, given a shared set of conditions. Ryan

(fewestmoves5 used a > different point system, but I have since changed it) > > The advantage of a points system for fewest moves challenges as with > other sports, is that some days will be easier than others. E.g. in > running, some days the wind can assist you, others it can hold you back. > Some days, you will get an easy cube scramble, others it will be > difficult (for humans). A points system assumes that everyone gets the > same advantage (i.e. same conditions), and only considers who was better > than whom, given a shared set of conditions. > > Ryan >

208. FMC rules
From: "Daniel Harris" <dan_j_harris@...>
To: <fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com>
Date: Fri, 2 Jun 2006 06:57:02 +0100

HI The policy is that you are not allowed to look up algorithms specifically for the purpose of FMC. If you can, imagine that you are in a real WCA competition scenario. All that you are allowed to have is pen and paper. The add in the Fewest Moves Companion, you can use that to help you since it doesn't calculate anything except for telling you how many moves you have used. You are NOT allowed to look up a PLL if you don't know it, and you are not allowed to use Cube Explorer or any type of computer program to calculate a solution from a particular position. Algorithms that you have learned previously for other purposes and that you know off by heart can be used. I am almost done with the new automated FMC system, I just have to do some testing and hopefully I will be able to launch the new pages just after the launch of the new challenge next week :) -----Original Message----- From: fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com [mailto:fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com] On Behalf Of fonnny Sent: 01 June 2006 17:38 To: fewestmoveschallenge@yahoogroups.com Subject: [fewestmoveschallenge] FMC rules So here's a question that I couldn't find answered anywhere. What's the policy on using aids / tables / charts? For example, at one point I had gotten it down to the PLL stage, and it came up to one of the PLLs I don't know off the top of my head. Am I allowed to look up what the appropriate PLL is? In a similar vein, at one point I was I believe 19 moves in, and had 3 c/e pairs on the f2l and the top edges oriented. I plugged it in to Cube Explorer and it gave me an 11 move solution from that point. Am I allowed to submit the total 30 move solution? thanks, (the other) dan. Yahoo! Groups Links
